  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer4952590512192424397">In a tiny Italian village, a young boy named Pietro is growing up tormented by bullies and ignored by his parents. When an aging playboy, Graziano Biglia, returns to town, a change is in the air: Pietro decides to take on the bullies, his lonely teacher Flora finds romance with the town’s prodigal son, and the inept janitor at the school proclaims his love for his favorite</span>
  <span id="freeText4952590512192424397" style="display:none">In a tiny Italian village, a young boy named Pietro is growing up tormented by bullies and ignored by his parents. When an aging playboy, Graziano Biglia, returns to town, a change is in the air: Pietro decides to take on the bullies, his lonely teacher Flora finds romance with the town’s prodigal son, and the inept janitor at the school proclaims his love for his favorite prostitute. But the village isn’t ready for such change, and when Graziano seduces and forgets Flora, both she and Pietro’s tentative hopes seem crushed forever. With great tenderness, Ammaniti shines light on the heart-wrenching failures and quiet redemptions of ordinary people trying to live extraordinary lives. <i>I’ll Steal You Away</i> is a fresh and classic story of a boy learning to be a man that delivers on the promise of Ammaniti’s acclaimed debut.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4952590512192424397" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62670479?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trivia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trivia" href="/user/show/2340808-trivia">Trivia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62670479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7381323816419528342">Let me start by saying that contemporary Italian literature somewhat scares me; I've been permanently scarred by some of the novels I've read. Ammaniti's <i>Io non ho paura (I'm not afraid)</i> was one of those; it left me with a strange intense feeling of discomfort I'm not really able to describe. But this one turned my whole view on Ammaniti and Italian prose upside down: it's a brilliant story with an unpredictable chain of events and it's packed with quirky colourful characters. Sure, the ending w</span>
  <span id="freeText7381323816419528342" style="display:none">Let me start by saying that contemporary Italian literature somewhat scares me; I've been permanently scarred by some of the novels I've read. Ammaniti's <i>Io non ho paura (I'm not afraid)</i> was one of those; it left me with a strange intense feeling of discomfort I'm not really able to describe. But this one turned my whole view on Ammaniti and Italian prose upside down: it's a brilliant story with an unpredictable chain of events and it's packed with quirky colourful characters. Sure, the ending was surprising and somewhat dissapointing, but strangely enough, it didn't ruin the whole experience for me. It just... sealed the plot in a most effective way. I wholeheartedly recommend this book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7381323816419528342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12460661425158420817">I devoured this book, just like the other two Ammaniti books i've recently read. And for good reason....this author knows how to tell a compelling and heart pulling story like very few other authors know how to do. Ammaniti takes the lives of ordinary people and tells it just how it is. In this book...the dreams and desires and fantasies of the characters take hold of you. I understand the multiple dreams and wishes that Graziano flits to and from like a butterfly from flower to flower. I know w</span>
  <span id="freeText12460661425158420817" style="display:none">I devoured this book, just like the other two Ammaniti books i've recently read. And for good reason....this author knows how to tell a compelling and heart pulling story like very few other authors know how to do. Ammaniti takes the lives of ordinary people and tells it just how it is. In this book...the dreams and desires and fantasies of the characters take hold of you. I understand the multiple dreams and wishes that Graziano flits to and from like a butterfly from flower to flower. I know what it's like to be the underdog that Pietro is. The characters in this book want a better life...they want to be happy...they want to become their definition of a success. But there is this thing called "reality"...and reality isnt's always a kind thing to the people living in Ischiano Scalo. On the front cover of this book is a quote by the London Times that says, "The new Italian word for talent is Ammaniti"...and that quote couldn't be more right.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1805142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10967415636311879836">I'LL STEAL YOU AWAY is the captivating and endearing tale of Ischiano Scalo's small-town residents, a kind of comic-tragic WINESBURG, OHIO. All of the characters in this novel demand your empathy: Once you start in on their troubles, their small victories, and their life-destroying defeats, the book is impossible to put down. And because this is Ammaniti, you can be sure that the children encounter something so disfiguring, yet so heartbreakingly human, that they can't help but be thrust into ad</span>
  <span id="freeText10967415636311879836" style="display:none">I'LL STEAL YOU AWAY is the captivating and endearing tale of Ischiano Scalo's small-town residents, a kind of comic-tragic WINESBURG, OHIO. All of the characters in this novel demand your empathy: Once you start in on their troubles, their small victories, and their life-destroying defeats, the book is impossible to put down. And because this is Ammaniti, you can be sure that the children encounter something so disfiguring, yet so heartbreakingly human, that they can't help but be thrust into adulthood. And meanwhile, the adults in their rural towns are not immune to the influx of cosmopolitan corruption (here in the form of "the most fuckable woman on the planet"). The result is a novel that is even more beautiful, picaresque, and haunting than the much-celebrated I'M NOT SCARED.</span>

<div id="review_886098446" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/886098446" />
    <a title="Kairi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8438367-kairi"><img alt="Kairi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1384268941p2/8438367.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/886098446?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kairi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kairi" href="/user/show/8438367-kairi">Kairi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788804476795" href="/book/show/1304611.Ti_prendo_e_ti_porto_via">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8438367-kairi?shelf=just-plain-disappointment">just-plain-disappointment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8438367-kairi?shelf=what-the-damn-hell">what-the-damn-hell</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8438367-kairi?shelf=italy">italy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8438367-kairi?shelf=nonsense-fest">nonsense-fest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer886098446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer561951527681931422">“Don’t judge a book by its cover” more like “don’t judge a book by its freaking title”, dammit. (But as for the English edition, the cover, too, since it’s really lovely). <br/><br/>Let me start with the most obvious thing: the writing. Especially in some parts, the writing was so less than average that you got the feeling any wannabe author could have written those lines, which left me deeply dumbfounded considering all the literary prizes the author has won over the years. Some examples of that were alo</span>
  <span id="freeText561951527681931422" style="display:none">“Don’t judge a book by its cover” more like “don’t judge a book by its freaking title”, dammit. (But as for the English edition, the cover, too, since it’s really lovely). <br /><br />Let me start with the most obvious thing: the writing. Especially in some parts, the writing was so less than average that you got the feeling any wannabe author could have written those lines, which left me deeply dumbfounded considering all the literary prizes the author has won over the years. Some examples of that were along the lines of: <br /><br /><i>- "His smile was like a sunny day in February."<br />- "His lacrimal glands were drier than the desert."<br />- "She was so cold and aloof, she must have swallowed a block of ice for breakfast". </i><br /><br />and so on. <br /><br />I mean.. <i>whaaat</i>? The only explanation I can come up with is that the dialogues, descriptions and everything else had to be consistent with the characters of a small village, so the poor writing was sort of done on purpose, but frankly that’s no excuse either, on the contrary if that’s the case it would be more of a sign of laziness than anything. <br /><br />Secondly, thank you, Mister author, for reminding me that humans are scum and that 90% of the time there’s no logic explanation behind their despicable actions and if there is one it’s really stupid, but I kinda already knew that. <br /><br />I think the characters were meant to be realistic, but they were a little <i>too</i> realistic, so they ended up being bad, clichéd caricatures.<br /><br />Also, the only two characters worth your attention, in the end are depicted as weak outcasts that end up being pushed in a corner or crashed either by society or other human beings. A very positive message indeed. (Now obviously books don’t necessarily have to give out an overall positive message and everything, but that doesn’t mean I can’t be pissed off by those that don’t, especially when they don’t make much sense). <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="561951527681931422" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/772177645?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allan" href="/user/show/25891345-allan">Allan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer772177645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12614913589700403146">This book has been on my shelf unread for probably 10 years, but I was motivated to pick it up due to its Italian author and setting, given that I was travelling in Italy.<br/><br/>To be honest, the novel was ok for me. It took me a while to work out where it was heading, as Ammaniti introduced several different seemingly unrelated characters, but, thanks to the nature of small town life, their lives become intertwined, if only briefly in some cases. I didn't particularly warm to, or identify with, any of</span>
  <span id="freeText12614913589700403146" style="display:none">This book has been on my shelf unread for probably 10 years, but I was motivated to pick it up due to its Italian author and setting, given that I was travelling in Italy.<br /><br />To be honest, the novel was ok for me. It took me a while to work out where it was heading, as Ammaniti introduced several different seemingly unrelated characters, but, thanks to the nature of small town life, their lives become intertwined, if only briefly in some cases. I didn't particularly warm to, or identify with, any of them - indeed there were a few who I actively detested, which I suppose was the author's intention. The twist in the novel was a little unexpected at the end, though the run up a little contrived.<br /><br />On hindsight, not really a holiday novel, given the area and subjects the book covered.<br /><br />All in all, it's been a while since I read any Ammaniti and on the strength of this novel I won't be going back to read any more any time soon.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111740046" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111740046?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841958248" href="/book/show/51018.I_ll_Steal_You_Away">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=wanderlust">wanderlust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=21st-centurylit">21st-centurylit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111740046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15689296801453830198">Pietro Moroni finds at the end of the school year that he's the only student in his class not to pass. The crushing blow sets the tone for the rest of the story and we're taken back six months to learn the events leading up to the failing. Additionally we meet Graziano Biglia, the small Italian town's prodigal son, who returns under his own set of extenuating circumstances. There are other characters as well, from Gloria, Pietro's best friend, to Flora Palmieri, Pietro's favorite teacher. Each o</span>
  <span id="freeText15689296801453830198" style="display:none">Pietro Moroni finds at the end of the school year that he's the only student in his class not to pass. The crushing blow sets the tone for the rest of the story and we're taken back six months to learn the events leading up to the failing. Additionally we meet Graziano Biglia, the small Italian town's prodigal son, who returns under his own set of extenuating circumstances. There are other characters as well, from Gloria, Pietro's best friend, to Flora Palmieri, Pietro's favorite teacher. Each of the characters has a different role in the story, and they all work together (separately) to bring around the events we learn in the beginning of the book are going to happen.<br /><br />I've seen comparisons to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80176.Winesburg__Ohio__Oxford_World_s_Classics_" title="Winesburg, Ohio (Oxford World's Classics) by Sherwood Anderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Winesburg, Ohio</a> which sort of makes me throw up a little in my mouth considering how little I like or appreciate Sherwood Anderson. But Ammaniti actually made me care for his characters, even the nasty ones. Anderson never made me give a hoot about any of Winesburg's inhabitants. <i>I'll Steal You Away</i> takes a similar concept - a small town and its people - but actually tells a complete story, with a beginning and an end. It begins with the promise that there's actually a point to the story, something I don't feel Anderson accomplished at all with <i>Winesburg</i>.<br /><br />This is the kind of story that deserves to be made into a movie and could actually be something.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15689296801453830198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer16062118130358930507">The book was gift, since the book merchant <b>knew</b> it was anything but literature... and gave it to me to learn what I'd think of it. Following a sane policy for avid readers, to put down a book after ten or so pages, one can imagine what was the outcome. If a writer (or scribbler) hasn't managed to present <i>living</i> characters or experiences (they could have happened in the present, past or future) to the <i>selective</i> readers' imagination, and to captivate their attention in the first chapter or... ten </span>
  <span id="freeText16062118130358930507" style="display:none">The book was gift, since the book merchant <b>knew</b> it was anything but literature... and gave it to me to learn what I'd think of it. Following a sane policy for avid readers, to put down a book after ten or so pages, one can imagine what was the outcome. If a writer (or scribbler) hasn't managed to present <i>living</i> characters or experiences (they could have happened in the present, past or future) to the <i>selective</i> readers' imagination, and to captivate their attention in the first chapter or... ten pages, then the book fails its purpose; no further reading, if not hop scotch-like, which unlikely will make of a blob (writing for the sake of... what?), a decent reading piece, let alone a masterpiece.<br /><br />What I think is actually immaterial. Today, so many books, worthless scraps, are hailed as literature from all sides, for umpteenth reasons (ideological? political? tribal?): hence cognitive and sensitive reading has no place in this one circumscribed world or venue, and would make no sense to most admirers of this ahem... novel, of which this reader doesn't either mean to disapprove or evaluate - different strokes for different folks, that's all. Or as they say in Italian: <i>il mondo è bello perché vario... ed <b>eventuale</b></i>. The last word is my contribution to the saying!</span>
  <a data-text-id="16062118130358930507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2088428029" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2088428029" />
    <a title="Leila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35938880-leila"><img alt="Leila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543402284p2/35938880.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2088428029?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leila" href="/user/show/35938880-leila">Leila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2088428029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9875995367847946215">Wowowow… Ok. so many things to say but so much to compress in a review.<br/>This novel, like so many written by modern italian authors in recent years, is dark, tragic, grotesque and… unsettling, i can add this. It’s unnerving. <br/>I add that i read this book in just 2 days. I utterly devoured it, for a few reasons. To start off, I think this novel is a representation of what Italian provincial life is, or at least of how Ammaniti perceives it: obviously in a stereotypical, exaggerated way, because Amman</span>
  <span id="freeText9875995367847946215" style="display:none">Wowowow… Ok. so many things to say but so much to compress in a review.<br />This novel, like so many written by modern italian authors in recent years, is dark, tragic, grotesque and… unsettling, i can add this. It’s unnerving. <br />I add that i read this book in just 2 days. I utterly devoured it, for a few reasons. To start off, I think this novel is a representation of what Italian provincial life is, or at least of how Ammaniti perceives it: obviously in a stereotypical, exaggerated way, because Ammaniti’s writing style is very imaginative, over the top and colourful. In fact, the characters are truly caricatures, caricatures of people Ammaniti must know/have known in real life. There is Graziano, the adventurous, good looking zen dude who’s stuck in his teenage years and is commitment phobic, Flora, the school teacher whose grey existence revolves around her ill mother and finally the poor little school boy Pietro, who comes from a highly dysfunctional background and reacts to this situation by simply, well, not reacting. And these are just a few examples. The list of characters is vast, often memorable in a negative way (Italo Miele, for one, and his policeman son Bruno) but THEY are the centre of the story, they make up the book, there is no true book plot that flows through it, almost as if Ammaniti has delighted himself in just describing in detail the numerous adventures and/or disadventures of a bunch of characters, who are somehow connected to the protagonists. For this reason the structure of the book reminded me of a series of movies and series that have gained immense popularity in italy and even abroad: one is the series "1993", which centres around characters that are somehow linked to each other, and very similarly are all pretty grotesque. the other is the academy award winning movie "The Great Beauty", which in my opinion resembles even more closely Ammaniti’s book. There is no true plot, just a bunch of bizarre people whose lives are intertwined and their ends are tragic. They have no goal, no apparent interest in life, they just survive the waves of misfortune by swimming and swimming, and then, eventually, sinking. Like this book, it’a a comedy of manners which looks closely at the numerous woes and flaws of its characters, many of them dark indeed, some less, but, as you say in italian, “nessuno is salva”. Their flaws are endlessly examined under a microscope, “nobody is saved”, and even more tragically, the ones who are truly good, out of the characters who aren’t at all (Pierini the bully, the Miele’s, Erica Trettel, the list goes on and on, many are the bad apples), are the ones who end up suffering the most. And what happens to the people you truly pine for throughout the book is perfectly explained by the Darwinian theory of evolution, something that was also applied by another Italian novelist, the Sicilian Giovanni Verga: the weaker ones, the kind ones, are overpowered, literally devoured by the strong and heartless ones. It’s tragic, but it’s inevitable, because that’s how nature works: simple. And this is a very common theme indeed in italian movies and literature, as cruel it may seem. It reflects a rather dark, undiscovered side of Italy, which is often concealed by tourism and and sometimes let into the light by the odd movie about the mafia in New York. So is this fundamental pessimism on existence a reflection of a society torn by injustices within the legal system and government? In Ischiano Scalo it is at least, since justice there is, in fact, virtually non existent. <br />The fact is, Ammaniti took on a great deal with this book, but ultimately his vibrant, lively and imaginative prose, which is what makes the book so entertaining, the list of varied and exaggerated characters, the post-apocalyptic, dark setting (a true specialty of the author), put this novel very high up in the ranks for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9875995367847946215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer39058035&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer39058035" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15629370881973191007">I desperately wanted to like I'll Steal You Away (mostly because I bought it in hardback), but I was disappointed. The book, which is set in Italy, tells the story of a little boy who is tormented by the other kids and the aging local lothario who moves back to town after his showgirl girlfriend breaks his heart. The two do come together in the end, but it mostly feels like you're reading two different books. Ammaniti also has the tendency to randomly dump pages of back story into the action of </span>
  <span id="freeText15629370881973191007" style="display:none">I desperately wanted to like I'll Steal You Away (mostly because I bought it in hardback), but I was disappointed. The book, which is set in Italy, tells the story of a little boy who is tormented by the other kids and the aging local lothario who moves back to town after his showgirl girlfriend breaks his heart. The two do come together in the end, but it mostly feels like you're reading two different books. Ammaniti also has the tendency to randomly dump pages of back story into the action of the novel. <br /><br />My favorite part of the book is when the lothario seduces the spinster school teacher. What can I say, I have a natural affinity for spinsters. But this part of the plot gets lost among the numerous sub-plots. I'm not the sort of reader who insists on a happy ending, but Ammaniti wreaks a gratuitous amount of misery on every single character. Overall, this book feels like the rough draft of a great novel. Sometimes I feel more frustrated reading a book that misses by an inch than I do when a book misses by a mile, because I wonder why no editor helped the author fix the problems.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7839151779839893298">I 'm not convinced that Ammaniti is an Italian literary superstar as he is referred in the book synopsis by his publisher, I 'm pretty sure that there are better writers out there in Italy without having read a lot of my neighbors literature. However it is always fun to read a book by him. Beware, although he has a very good sense of humor, his books aren't what we usually call funny. He writes in absolutely everyday language, like we most of people are talking to each other and this makes him v</span>
  <span id="freeText7839151779839893298" style="display:none">I 'm not convinced that Ammaniti is an Italian literary superstar as he is referred in the book synopsis by his publisher, I 'm pretty sure that there are better writers out there in Italy without having read a lot of my neighbors literature. However it is always fun to read a book by him. Beware, although he has a very good sense of humor, his books aren't what we usually call funny. He writes in absolutely everyday language, like we most of people are talking to each other and this makes him very familiar to a reader. His storylines are really catchy and enjoyable, make someone to relax and have a cool time reading him, his language is really fresh and easy. However, do not expect something deep, you will be disappointed. It is the fourth book I have read by him, personally I think it pales comparing to the previous I have tried so far. Not a bad book actually, but not something special either and far away from memorable. Ammaniti's work for me is like a perfect reading break after something heavy and difficult or after a bad book. And don't get this wrong, we need these kind of books from time to time.</span>

Amazing characters wrapped up in a gut-wrenching plot that did not let me go until it wrung every emotional response possible from my willing psyche. This is a work that does not bother with superfluities; anything that might distract from the inevitability of the superbly-crafted story is stripped, leaving pure narrative. I can't say enough positive things about this book.</span>

<div id="review_3210224458" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3210224458" />
    <a title="Rebecca Alcazaze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41381763-rebecca-alcazaze"><img alt="Rebecca Alcazaze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594896854p2/41381763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3210224458?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca Alcazaze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca Alcazaze" href="/user/show/41381763-rebecca-alcazaze">Rebecca Alcazaze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841957746" href="/book/show/432416.I_ll_Steal_You_Away">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3210224458&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3210224458" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13802468292207860890">I’m a bit sad I have to give this a 1 star review as the story itself was well paced and readable, with a vivid sense of place and some beautiful little details. I also remember enjoying Ammaniti’s ‘I’m not Scared’ when I read that some years ago.<br/><br/>Sadly, elements of this are just too questionable.<br/><br/>Over the first hundred pages or so I excused some of the naff language and dated racial/gender terms, putting it down to translation. Continued use of questionable language (stuff like ‘coloured’ and ‘po</span>
  <span id="freeText13802468292207860890" style="display:none">I’m a bit sad I have to give this a 1 star review as the story itself was well paced and readable, with a vivid sense of place and some beautiful little details. I also remember enjoying Ammaniti’s ‘I’m not Scared’ when I read that some years ago.<br /><br />Sadly, elements of this are just too questionable.<br /><br />Over the first hundred pages or so I excused some of the naff language and dated racial/gender terms, putting it down to translation. Continued use of questionable language (stuff like ‘coloured’ and ‘poof’) stood out but I put it down to a blurring between narrative voice and POVs of characters with shitty world views. As a rule I always try to read things in context. I’m rarely offended by language and/or subject matter that has just aged incredibly badly. <br /><br />But ‘Steal You Away’ then goes on to feature a scene in which a virgin school teacher is isolated, conned, spiked, undressed while unconscious in a car, and ‘de-flowered’ in a public spa pool. Her Fabio-encoded rapist (and let’s be clear - that’s definitely what he is, a rapist) does question some of his actions (particularly for attempting to force ‘sodomy’ on his drugged victim) but the narrative never truly vilifies him. The use of date-rape drugs to take advantage of his quarry is painted as merely ‘unsporting’.<br /><br />After the teacher character’s painful al fresco ordeal the narrator explains how....<br /><br /> ‘She touched her breasts, her legs [. . .] Despite<br /> the bruises &amp; scratches, her body seemed firmer <br /> fuller, and those aches showed that it was alive <br /> &amp; responded well to such stimuli. It was a body<br /> suited to sex.’<br /><br />The notion that an unsatisfactory virgin body can be unlocked to a world of new possibilities by a sleazy predator’s drug-rape intrusion is beyond offensive to me... and I read so much old, un-politically correct stuff that I rarely register true offence.<br /><br />Yes, all the characters are punished in some way in this narrative - but the tone with which the narrator (I’m reluctant to call out Ammaniti here on the minuscule chance that he’s purposely created an implied narrator with offensive ideas about woman and rape culture) discusses women; as sacks of meat and goo intended to either attract or repel men, is amplified by this rape scene. <br /><br />Written in 1999 and translated into English in 2006, it could never be old enough for this to be OK. I’d be surprised if anyone could read this scene of rape as awakening in any positive way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13802468292207860890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3270469574" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3270469574" />
    <a title="Jan Wauters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/112834423-jan-wauters"><img alt="Jan Wauters" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_536305181" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="vorbore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="vorbore" href="/user/show/1862898-vorbore">vorbore</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789531742849" href="/book/show/6333794-pokupit-u-te-i-odvesti">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1862898-vorbore?shelf=other">other</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer536305181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer466351160898080698">I actually finished this two days ago, but I did not feel able to compose a comprehensive review (not that I feel able now) and I think that this is not a book that deserves to be closed without a word. <br/>The feeling I had while reading this book was as if I was a bystander to a disaster, so similar to the feeling I had in the beginning of "Memory" by Lois McMaster Bujold <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10374167.Pam_enje__Vorkosigan_Saga___10_' title='Pamćenje (Vorkosigan Saga, #10) by Lois McMaster Bujold' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pamćenje</a>. The helpless frozen feeling, when you see someone going backwards ever closer to the brink of a cliff, but you can't </span>
  <span id="freeText466351160898080698" style="display:none">I actually finished this two days ago, but I did not feel able to compose a comprehensive review (not that I feel able now) and I think that this is not a book that deserves to be closed without a word. <br />The feeling I had while reading this book was as if I was a bystander to a disaster, so similar to the feeling I had in the beginning of "Memory" by Lois McMaster Bujold <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10374167.Pam_enje__Vorkosigan_Saga___10_" title="Pamćenje (Vorkosigan Saga, #10) by Lois McMaster Bujold" rel="nofollow noopener">Pamćenje</a>. The helpless frozen feeling, when you see someone going backwards ever closer to the brink of a cliff, but you can't even yell "Stop!". The helpless anger that makes me want to reach into the book, grab the protagonist, shake them and yell "NO!!! WRONG!!!" in their ear. So many small wrong turns that lead to catastrophes. So many blunt, self-absorbed, aimless, puerile persons, regardless of their actual age. So many squandered lives. And yet the book ends with hope. That is a sort of comforting, at the first glance. But that is hope just for one of many, then I remember all the others in this book whose chance for hope is irretrievably lost. What a horrible view, but sadly also one that feels so believable.<br /> <br />I am amazed by the way this book is written (yay to the translator as well). The writing kept me with this book all the way, however the actual pattern of events and the atmosphere repelled me. And, with all the predominating feeling of doom, still this book managed to make me laugh out loud at times. <br /><br />This feeling of oncoming disaster would probably be less strong if I would have read this book when I was younger. I should have read this book when I was younger, less sensitive to wrong decisions that seem so right at the time they are made, then it would not crush me so much. Then I would read it as a study of a small isolated backwards community that offers little to none opportunities to it's inhabitants, reducing them to lesser people, and I would conclude that it does not actually concern me.<br /><br />This book beats you to the head with a large hammer that has written all over it: if you chose an action, you definitely chose the consequence of that action as well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="466351160898080698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_103669600" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/103669600" />
    <a title="Neil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1666671-neil"><img alt="Neil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442473216p2/1666671.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103669600?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neil" href="/user/show/1666671-neil">Neil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1666671-neil?shelf=a-to-z-project">a-to-z-project</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103669600" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6291497591990200800">A to Z Project, Book 13<br/>Ammaniti's novel starts like an Italian village movie (Amarcord or Cinema Paradiso, for example) but one set in the contemporary era. Three characters get the majority of his attention. Pietro Moroni is a small boy, friendless except for Gloria, a wealthy, beautiful tomboy whose parents have always liked his calming influence. They're close friends, but he's in love with her. Pietro is having trouble with a trio of local bullies.<br/><br/>Graziano Biglia is a singer who does contemp</span>
  <span id="freeText6291497591990200800" style="display:none">A to Z Project, Book 13<br />Ammaniti's novel starts like an Italian village movie (Amarcord or Cinema Paradiso, for example) but one set in the contemporary era. Three characters get the majority of his attention. Pietro Moroni is a small boy, friendless except for Gloria, a wealthy, beautiful tomboy whose parents have always liked his calming influence. They're close friends, but he's in love with her. Pietro is having trouble with a trio of local bullies.<br /><br />Graziano Biglia is a singer who does contemporary gypsy music and flamenco (like the Gipsy Kings). But he's tired of the road and a desire to return to his hometown (where he is a legendary lothario) and open a jeans shop is growing. At first, he dreams that it will be with his vapid television spokesmodel girlfriend, but when she dumps him, Graziano suddenly decides to make a play for a local schoolteacher.<br /><br />Flora Palmieri is Pietro's teacher, the target of Graziano's sudden attentions, and also the target of the same bullies (her students) that are bothering Pietro. She's a quiet woman who secretly tends her disabled mother at home. She's beautiful, but completely inexperienced with men.<br /><br />The novel documents life in the village and the converging stories of the three lead characters. It takes an unexpectedly ugly turn in the last fifty pages that shocked me a bit. Looking back, I think it is reasonably well motivated, but this is the kind of story that one expects to turn out differently. <br /><br />Ammaniti is a great storyteller. I was caught up in the story quickly and never lost interest for a moment. In addition to the very believable leads, he builds at least twenty other characters, sometimes even telling his tale from their point of view for a few pages. Some are hilarious, some pathetic, some frightening, but none of them fail to capture attention. <br /><br />This isn't a perfect book, but it's very strong and will definitely stay with me. In a world full of books that often feel very similar to other stories, this is a true original.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6291497591990200800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen" href="/user/show/7257685-karen">Karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer992074320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4851608307276054721">I had to use a little willpower to read this all the way through. This is the second translated-from-Italian novel I have started this year, and the second that really didn't thrill me. (The other was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels__1_' title='My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels #1) by Elena Ferrante' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Brilliant Friend</a>, and I didn't finish that one.) I am starting to think that perhaps I just don't like to read books in translation. I kept getting hung up on language. I would read a phrase and think, "What are all the meanings of the phrase that is translated from? What nuances am I missing?"<br/><br/>I</span>
  <span id="freeText4851608307276054721" style="display:none">I had to use a little willpower to read this all the way through. This is the second translated-from-Italian novel I have started this year, and the second that really didn't thrill me. (The other was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13586707.My_Brilliant_Friend__The_Neapolitan_Novels__1_" title="My Brilliant Friend (The Neapolitan Novels #1) by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">My Brilliant Friend</a>, and I didn't finish that one.) I am starting to think that perhaps I just don't like to read books in translation. I kept getting hung up on language. I would read a phrase and think, "What are all the meanings of the phrase that is translated from? What nuances am I missing?"<br /><br />I'm not philosophically anti-translation, certainly. I just don't *enjoy* reading it. At least, not these two.<br /><br />I made it through this one because there were two storylines, and when I got bored with one the other would come along and be a change. At least, I guess that's why.<br /><br />Maybe, like sewing, translated fiction is something I will be able to make friends with 10-20 years from now, when (as I fondly imagine) my life will not be so busy. Hope springs eternal!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4851608307276054721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10765676410359700782">Really hot days in Melbourne so good day to get into a good book. Read "Steal You Away" in 1 day and thoroughly enjoyed it. It contains humour, tragedy but also sex, violence, Nigerian prostitues, date-rape drugs, dead donkeys and catapults. The book is set in the Italian town of Ischiano Scalo, a backwater swampy village. The two main characters are Pietro Moroni, a 12 year old boy who is the product of an alcoholic father and a depressed, brutalised mother. He is sensitive intelligent, a loner</span>
  <span id="freeText10765676410359700782" style="display:none">Really hot days in Melbourne so good day to get into a good book. Read "Steal You Away" in 1 day and thoroughly enjoyed it. It contains humour, tragedy but also sex, violence, Nigerian prostitues, date-rape drugs, dead donkeys and catapults. The book is set in the Italian town of Ischiano Scalo, a backwater swampy village. The two main characters are Pietro Moroni, a 12 year old boy who is the product of an alcoholic father and a depressed, brutalised mother. He is sensitive intelligent, a loner and born victim. The other principal character is Graziano Biglio, (who won the Casanova Cup by bedding more than 300 women), who is left in the lurch when the love of his life, Erica, a go-go dancer, dumps him. There are a number of memorable characters and the author plays comedy against tragedy.</span>

<div id="review_2579114653" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2579114653?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2579114653" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2961410012508152363">This book was hard to rate. The writing was good, often charming and funny. However, I was too taken aback by elements of the plot to give this a high rating. Slipping drugs in a woman's drink to seduce her is probably the topper. The story revolves around a bullied boy who has filed a course that would allow him to get out of junior high. He seems destined to be caught in the same cycle that entraps his angry father, his withdrawn mother and dim-witted brother. His solution to get out of his tr</span>
  <span id="freeText2961410012508152363" style="display:none">This book was hard to rate. The writing was good, often charming and funny. However, I was too taken aback by elements of the plot to give this a high rating. Slipping drugs in a woman's drink to seduce her is probably the topper. The story revolves around a bullied boy who has filed a course that would allow him to get out of junior high. He seems destined to be caught in the same cycle that entraps his angry father, his withdrawn mother and dim-witted brother. His solution to get out of his troubles is a big surprise, but also a troubling aspect of the plot. There are funny scenes in this book, the catapult comes to mind, but not enough to overcome aspects of it that I found repulsive. </span>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2929155097" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5662485569541277094">Extremely compelling read about an innocent Italian village boy who is constantly bullied. The author paints clear pictures of the village and the villagers. The boy, Pietro Montini sets off a sequence of events that change the course of his life forever. Woven into this story is that of Graziano Biglio, a local Romeo who finally finds what he thinks is true love only for his rash decisions to snatch it away from him.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iJxmDLwXdxRIFG559fMsOWP8YlCs7PWbP1Zh8dDG5zR2ORuLmoI+ob01O36CIvKIQHf5nMdTVhv9Kn342XxNXg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fC669k1Y+5OXT7Z5pao4cJ3ebocqe0Ag/s9S05pRYQuCi8dxa82yJmJu437Se+bBvlX1S0HE46A8s07ak+vLYQ==')})
  }

And Pietro plunges deeper into the depths of his life before seeing the simple truth before his eyes.\n\nSandro Veronesi makes art of every detail, creating a mosaic of humor, hope,profound insight, and emotional resonance. La bambina è inizialmente convinta che Bonaria Urrai faccia la sarta, e infatti le giornate sono segnate dallo scorrere nella bottega casalinga di una umanità paesana, fatta di piccole miserie e di relazioni costruite di gesti e di sguardi, molto piú che di parole. Accettata come normale dal paese, l’adozione solidale tra la vecchia e la bambina si consolida malgrado lo sfaldarsi circostante delle antiche certezze. Attraverso lo sguardo privilegiato della bambina che cresce, le contraddizioni tra il vecchio e il nuovo emergono via via piú evidenti: nell’esperienza della scuola dell’obbligo, e in quella del confronto tra la fede cristiana e i retaggi di una religiosità assai piú antica nel tempo. Ad attenderla all\'aeroporto, Gojko, poeta bosniaco, amico fratello, amore mancato, che ai tempi festosi delle Olimpiadi invernali del 1984 traghettò Gemma verso l\'amore della sua vita, Diego, il fotografo di pozzanghere.\nIl romanzo racconta la storia di questo amore, una storia di ragazzi farneticanti che si rincontrano oggi, giovani sprovveduti, invecchiati in un dopoguerra recente. Una storia d\'amore appassionata, imperfetta come gli amori veri.\nMa anche la storia di una maternità cercata, negata, risarcita. Il cammino misterioso di una nascita che fa piazza pulita della scienza, della biologia, e si addentra nella placenta preistorica di una Guerra che mentre uccide procrea.\nIn questo grande affresco di tenebra e luce, in questo romanzo intimo e sociale, le voci di quei ragazzi si accordano e si frantumano nel continuo rimando tra il ventre di Gemma e il ventre della città dilaniata. Ma l\'avventura di Gemma e Diego è anche la storia di tutti noi, perché Margaret Mazzantini ha scritto un coraggioso romanzo contemporaneo. Di pace e di guerra.\nLa pace è l\'aridità fumosa di un Occidente flaccido di egoismi, perso nella salamoia del benessere. La guerra è quella di una donna che ingaggia contro la natura una battaglia estrema e oltraggiosa. L\'assedio di Sarajevo diventa l\'assedio di ogni personaggio di questa vicenda di non eroi scaraventati dal calcio della Storia in un destino che sembra in attesa di loro come un tiratore scelto. Il cammino intimo di un uomo e di una donna verso un figlio, il loro viaggio di iniziazione alla paternità e alla maternità diventa un travaglio epico, una favola dura come l\'ingiustizia, luminosa come un miracolo.\nDopo Non ti muovere, con una scrittura che è cifra inconfondibile di identità letteraria, Margaret Mazzantini ci regala un romanzo-mondo, opera trascinante e di forte impegno etico, spiazzante come un thriller, emblematica come una parabola. Una catarsi che dimostra come attraverso tutto il male della Storia possa erompere lo stupore smagato, sereno, di un nuovo principio.\nUna specie di avvento che ha il volto mobile, le membra lunghe e ancora sgraziate, l\'ombrosità e gli slanci di un figlio di oggi chiamato Pietro.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"689313661394552605\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Una casa, una famiglia, il passato, un\'idea di futuro, la nostra peggiore fotografia impressa a fuoco negli occhi di chi abbiamo amato. Passiamo la vita a spaccare vasi e incollare cocci illudendoci di essere nuovi di zecca. E cerchiamo di non guardare troppo indietro, perché il tempo dei bilanci è un tempo vano, ridicolo e struggente. Domenico Starnone ha scritto un libro intensissimo e vero. Il racconto a tre voci delle forze sotterranee che tengono in vita i matrimoni anche dopo l\'amore.\n\n«Se tu te ne sei scordato, egregio signore, te lo ricordo io: sono tua moglie». Si apre cosí la lettera che Vanda scrive al marito che se n\'è andato di casa, lasciandola in preda a una tempesta di rabbia impotente e domande che non trovano risposta. Si sono sposati giovani all\'inizio degli anni Sessanta, per desiderio di indipendenza, ma poi attorno a loro il mondo è cambiato, e ritrovarsi a trent\'anni con una famiglia a carico è diventato un segno di arretratezza piú che di autonomia. Perciò adesso lui se ne sta a Roma, innamorato della grazia lieve di una sconosciuta con cui i giorni sono sempre gioiosi, e lei a Napoli con i figli, a misurare l\'estensione del silenzio e il crescere dell\'estraneità. Che cosa siamo disposti a sacrificare, pur di non sentirci in trappola? E che cosa perdiamo, quando scegliamo di tornare sui nostri passi? Perché niente è piú radicale dell\'abbandono, ma niente è piú tenace di quei lacci invisibili che legano le persone le une alle altre. E a volte basta un gesto minimo per far riaffiorare quello che abbiamo provato a mettere da parte. Domenico Starnone ci regala una storia emozionante e fortissima, il racconto magistrale di una fuga, di un ritorno, di tutti i fallimenti, quelli che ci sembrano insuperabili e quelli che ci fanno <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7048500895464966644">Niccolò Ammaniti was born in Rome in 1966. He has written three novels and a collection of short stories. He won the prestigious Italian Viareggio-Repaci Prize for Fiction with his bestselling novel I'm Not Scared, which has been translated into thirty-five languages.</span>
